#Habitat#
State waterfowl refuge along the margins of USACE flood control reservoir. Strip along the lake margins ~1km wide of moist soil grassland with mix of forbs, native warm season grasses (Andropogon, Schyzachyrium, Eragrostis, Aristida, etc.), and some sparse woody vegetation (Cephalanthus, Quercus saplings, etc.) bounded by oak hickory forest and, in places, pine plantation.  Three or four shallow waterfowl impoundments partially flooded in the winter.

#Editing#
FFT filter in Adobe Audition, Blackman window, 2048 pts, 400Hz-14kHz bandpass.

#Habitat#
State waterfowl refuge along the margins of USACE flood control reservoir. Strip along the lake margins ~1km wide of moist soil grassland with mix of forbs, native warm season grasses (Andropogon, Schyzachyrium, Eragrostis, Aristida, etc.), and some sparse woody vegetation (Cephalanthus, Quercus saplings, etc.) bounded by oak hickory forest and, in places, pine plantation.  Three or four shallow waterfowl impoundments partially flooded in the winter." "yes" "no" NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA

Rinchen Boardman and I came across this bird and puzzled over its identity for several minutes, before finally getting visual confirmation of it, and watching it sing its strange song through our binoculars.

Remarkably, this appears to be the first recording of White-crowned Sparrow from Labrador in the xeno-canto archive. However, it by no means is a typical song from this region. We heard many other White-crowned Sparrows in Goose Bay and on the eastern shore near Mary's Harbour, etc., that hardly resemble the bizarre monotonous song preserved in this recording." This dialect is very different from the birds in the Willamette Valley to the coast, but it's an astonishingly close match to recordings from the Puget Sound area and Vancouver Island. Yellow bill, narrower white lateral head stripes, and pale gray lores and supraloral were obvious in the field. Oriantha does not occur anywhere near here, despite the range map here, which is incorrect. The tongue extending down from NE Oregon should stop east of Bend and Prineville (not connect to the Cascades) and then extend southward from there to connect to the north-central and northeastern California populations. And considering the dialect of this bird, there should be a narrow finger extending southward through the Washington and Oregon Cascades." "yes" "yes" NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA

This is a call note that is different than the migratory Z.l pugetensis; I observed the bird as it repeatedly made the call.

There are 2 adults and 1 immature bird at this location, an closed nursery, approximately 2 miles from the nearest shoreline.  These birds are across the street from my work, and I heard them for the first time last year (after being at this location for 33 years).  The recording was made from the top of an 8 foot fence along a busy street. The main vegetation is low ornamental shrubs, with Pittosporum the only species that I could identify.  The old nursery has shrubbery inside the fence, and the birds fly back and forth from there to a large tree across the street.  Adult bird had black and white head stripes, a yellow bill, streaked back, and a short primary projection." "yes" "no" "Pipilo maculatus" "Corvus brachyrhynchos" NA NA NA NA NA NA NA NA
